<div id="review_1136264627" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1136264627" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1136264627?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1136264627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11319267360415167151"><b>Q. What if _____?</b><br/><b>A. Nearly Everyone Would Die!</b><br/>- Randall Munroe, What If?<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463624825i/19139712._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='301' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>There are certain things on this planet that you seemed to do fine without, but as soon as you discover, you can't now do without. Diet Dr. Pepper, David Foster Wallace, dark chocolate covered cherries all fit into this category. So, too, does Randall Munroe. He seems to occupy a space near, but not on, that vacated by Gary Larson when the great Gary Larson stopped drawing <i>the Far Side</i> (January 1, 1995). If you are unimpres</span>
  <span id="freeText11319267360415167151" style="display:none"><b>Q. What if _____?</b><br><b>A. Nearly Everyone Would Die!</b><br>- Randall Munroe, What If?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463624825i/19139712._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="301" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>There are certain things on this planet that you seemed to do fine without, but as soon as you discover, you can't now do without. Diet Dr. Pepper, David Foster Wallace, dark chocolate covered cherries all fit into this category. So, too, does Randall Munroe. He seems to occupy a space near, but not on, that vacated by Gary Larson when the great Gary Larson stopped drawing <i>the Far Side</i> (January 1, 1995). If you are unimpressed by Gary Larsen or <i>the Far Side</i> or do not know who he is or what I'm talking about, hell man, read no further. This book is not for you. I'm not trying to suggest that Gary Larson and Randall Munroe occupy the same ground. They are very different. Their approach to science is different. Their technique. However, the Venn Diagram of those readers of Gary Larson 20+ years ago would closely resemble those readers of Randall Munroe's <a href="http://xkcd.com" rel="nofollow noopener">xkcd</a>. <br><br>Nerds. <br><br>They are both worshiped by nerds. They are nerd gods. In this godhead of scientific nerd entertainers also exists Bill Nye. Anyway, these are binary science artists. You either get them and love them or you don't. If you don't, well congrats, I really hope someday you will recognized what you did to our beautiful world by voting for Trump. Bastard.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463624825i/19139713._SX540_.png" width="400" height="339" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Anyway, this book. This book is filled with drawings and explanations by Randall Munroe on topics as diverse as:<br><br>"<b>Q.</b> <i>What would happen if you tried to hit a baseball pitched at 90 percent the speed of light?</i>"<br><br>"<b>Q.</b> <i>What would happen if you were to gather a mole (unit of measurement) of moles (the small furry critter) in one place?</i>"<br><br>"<b>Q.</b> <i>How much Force power can Yoda output?</i>"<br><br>"<b>Q.</b> <i>How much Force power can Yoda output?</i>"<br><br>"<b>Q.</b> <i>How high can a human throw something?</i>"<br><br>Here is the magic of this book, and why it is relevant and important. It is the essentials of science. Science is always jumping into absurd places asking weird questions. Monroe capture the joy of this experience and he integrates the reader into it. He is a translator (like most scientists are) of complex methods into a narrative of explanation. He just takes several absurd, but still logical steps further. <br><br>I find his book about one standard deviation better than similar attempts at similar things. I'm thinking of Leyner's books: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/131529.Why_Do_Men_Have_Nipples__Hundreds_of_Questions_You_d_Only_Ask_a_Doctor_After_Your_Third_Martini" title="Why Do Men Have Nipples? Hundreds of Questions You'd Only Ask a Doctor After Your Third Martini by Mark Leyner" rel="nofollow noopener">Why Do Men Have Nipples?: Hundreds of Questions You'd Only Ask a Doctor After Your Third Martini</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6611.Why_Do_Men_Fall_Asleep_After_Sex__More_Questions_You_d_Only_Ask_a_Doctor_After_Your_Third_Whiskey_Sour" title="Why Do Men Fall Asleep After Sex? More Questions You'd Only Ask a Doctor After Your Third Whiskey Sour by Mark Leyner" rel="nofollow noopener">Why Do Men Fall Asleep After Sex? More Questions You'd Only Ask a Doctor After Your Third Whiskey Sour</a>. Both are using humor and science and the strategy of funky questions. There are a couple differences, that matter. Mark Leyner isn't a scientist. He's a soft postmodernist author that is playing doctor explaining awkward questons. Monroe is a scientist that is using the scientific method and humor to explain absurd, and sometimes practically nonsense questions. While both of these books can be considered humor books, I tend to favor the one written by a scientist who can draw (kinda) dinosaurs and a pyramid of giraffes. Personal preference I guess. <br><br>Anyway, if you or anyone in your family is a nerd, or raising a nerd, and enjoy absurdity and funky questions, well, this is almost a perfect book. If not, go ahead and try and to convince me that your vote for Trump isn't going to be considered the beginning of the end of our civilization. You have no soul. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11319267360415167151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1814543808?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/695116-jim">Jim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1814543808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11439921752644818760">Update: He published a lot more that aren't in this book. Read them for free here:<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://what-if.xkcd.com/archive/'>http://what-if.xkcd.com/archive/</a><br/><br/>So... I love xkcd & some of these questions were really interesting - funny on the face with some really interesting science & math backing up the answer. For instance, the bullet being hit by lightning got into quite a few interesting facts about lightning. Who wouldn't want to know if a machine gun jet pack would work including discussions on caliber, barrel burn out, & fire rates?</span>
  <span id="freeText11439921752644818760" style="display:none">Update: He published a lot more that aren't in this book. Read them for free here:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://what-if.xkcd.com/archive/">http://what-if.xkcd.com/archive/</a><br /><br />So... I love xkcd &amp; some of these questions were really interesting - funny on the face with some really interesting science &amp; math backing up the answer. For instance, the bullet being hit by lightning got into quite a few interesting facts about lightning. Who wouldn't want to know if a machine gun jet pack would work including discussions on caliber, barrel burn out, &amp; fire rates? Cooking a steak via a high altitude drop wasn't nearly as crazy as a mole of moles, but they would make an interesting planetoid. I never would have thought of that on my own. <br /><br />This isn't a book to just sit down &amp; read. The questions &amp; answers often require contemplation. Yoda powered houses probably won't catch on, but discovering just what his maximum power output was when he picked Luke's X-wing out of the swamp got into equations about power &amp; how many Yodas it would take to power our civilization. Sure, I learned them in 9th grade physics, but the refresher was a whole lot more interesting than Ms. Hummel's class ever was. <i>(Sorry, Ms. Hummel. Your class was interesting &amp; figuring out horsepower by running up a flight of stairs was great, but... well... Yoda. Yes, I know your class was long before even the first Star Wars movie, but... Yoda-power. How green can you get?)</i><br /><br />And then there are the <b>Weird &amp; Worrying Questions</b> sections that dotted this odd amalgamation. <br /><i>Would it be possible to get your teeth to such a cold temperature that they would shatter upon drinking a hot cup of coffee?</i> is fairly horrifying, but whatever turns you on. <br /><i>Is it possible to cry so much you dehydrate yourself?</i> is sad, but they're still pure entertainment for me. <br /><br />If I were Munroe &amp; had to worry about the people sending those emails, I might well disconnect from society &amp; move to the middle of northern Alberta, Canada. You think I exaggerate? <br /><i>How many houses are burned down in the United States every year? What would be the easiest way to increase that number by a significant amount (say, at least 15%)?</i> <br /><i>How fast would a human have to run in order to be cut in half at the bellybutton by a cheese-cutting wire?</i> Eeek! There are some scary people out there!<br /><br />Highly recommended in small doses!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11439921752644818760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9409906528279591379" style="display:none">My boyfriend has never heard of XKCD. So I'm newly single, and looking for some fun.... <br /><br />...book recommendations. But to my dismay, I don't think that he fully appreciated the genius of it, but well... some people are colorblind. Life is sometimes cruel like that. <br /><br />Anyway, this book was great. I've been a fan of XKCD for a long time now, and even if this book was crap, I'd have happily purchased a copy just to support Randall Munroe for my previous webcomic enjoyment. But this book was not crap. It was great. Interesting question content, well-thought out and researched answers, just the right mixture of humor to balance out the brain 'splody math stuff... I loved it. <br /><br />I'm going to give this book three awards: <br />First, for best comedic use of [citation needed]. <br /><br />Second, for best usage of footnote text EVAR. (Really, I couldn't imagine reading this in ebook format - the footnotes made me giggle so frequently that if they were a pain in the tushie to read on an ebook, so much greatness would be missed.)<br /><br />Finally, for best travelogue. I've always wanted to visit The Netherlands. ;)</span>

    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1285142346?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=ignorance-of-experts">ignorance-of-experts</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1285142346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18195346465739251133">This book is a 303 page answer to the question: “What would happen if a skilled mathematician with an internet connection fielded unusual science questions?” The answer seems to be a best-seller. Munroe has recently announced that he will follow this success with a sequel, <i>How To</i>; and this leads us naturally to consider whether this will turn into a trilogy. If so, we can already begin to predict how the last installment will be named.<br/><br/>If we assume that the title will, like its predecessors, cons</span>
  <span id="freeText18195346465739251133" style="display:none">This book is a 303 page answer to the question: “What would happen if a skilled mathematician with an internet connection fielded unusual science questions?” The answer seems to be a best-seller. Munroe has recently announced that he will follow this success with a sequel, <i>How To</i>; and this leads us naturally to consider whether this will turn into a trilogy. If so, we can already begin to predict how the last installment will be named.<br /><br />If we assume that the title will, like its predecessors, consist of two words, the range of possibilities is quite large. A back of the envelope calculation suggests that the total number of unique two-word combinations in English is over 29 billion. If we then make the assumption that one of these words has to be a question word (who, what, where…) then the list is still massive—well over one million. We can further restrict possibilities: first, to short words (one or two syllables); and, second, to idiomatic English expressions. Unfortunately, the list is still quite long. Instead of calculating exactly how long, however, I will make some concrete guesses as to which titles I think are most promising.<br /><br /><i>Why Not?: Carefree Acquiescence to Very Bad Suggestions</i><br /><br />Q: Want to go swimming and then climb that telephone pole?<br /><br />A: Hmmm. Okay, why not?<br /><br /><br /><i>What Now?: Misheard Statements by the Partially Deaf</i><br /><br />Q: I think we’re having moussaka for dinner.<br /><br />A: Did you just say Mustafa foreigner?<br /><br /><br /><i>Why Me?: Self-pitying Laments by the Emotionally Immature</i><br /><br />Q: I can’t believe that Annie cheated on me with Brian! I loved her!<br /><br />A: That was seventeen years ago, in junior high school.<br /><br /><br /><i>How About?: Casual Suggestions for Common Activities</i><br /><br />Q: How about we go bowling?<br /><br />A: Sure.<br /><br /><br /><i>Where Is?: Convoluted Directions for Lost Travelers</i><br /><br />Q: Excuse me, do you know how I can find the Expo Center?<br /><br />A: The Expo Center? Well, first you take the second right up ahead. Keep going for about five minutes until you see a big blue sign. Don’t turn there, but turn left two intersections after that. Then you’ll hit a roundabout. Keep straight going until you see a sign for exit 31. Make sure you’re in the right lane before that. Now, this is the tricky part after you take the exit, you’re going to have to reenter the highway going the other direction… <br /><br /><br /><i>What About?: Evasive Answers for Harried Politicians</i><br /><br />Q: Is it true that you that you were briefly a member of the KKK?<br /><br />A: I don’t see why everyone is so focused on this. You guys in the media are so shallow! What about the new tax reform bill I’ve proposed? <br /><br /><br /><i>What For?: Nihilistic Answers to Polite Requests</i><br /><br />Q: Can you pass the salt?<br /><br />A: I suppose I can, but really what's the point? We eat to experience a tiny vanishing pleasure and to sustain our fleeting existence. But what does all this accomplish? Nothing.<br />________<br />These are just my hunches. Do you have any? Feel free to share it in the comments.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18195346465739251133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1263102451?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Junta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Junta" href="/user/show/38230974-junta">Junta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=american">american</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1263102451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7407489300064149717"><u>
  <b><i>What If?</i>, or Serendipity; or the Literary/Pop Culture Quotes Quiz</b>
</u><br/><br/>Late at night, about a week ago, I went through the Top Quotes pages of Goodreads. A hundred pages, each with thirty quotes: that's 3000 quotes. I have problems. I was searching for quotes that included (on the most part, primarily consisted of) a line in the form of 'If A, B' (let's call it an <i>If</i> quote, or IQ) - I'd compiled them because I had a certain idea for a review where I would use them.<br/><br/>What's the catch? The idea was fo</span>
  <span id="freeText7407489300064149717" style="display:none"><u>
  <b><i>What If?</i>, or Serendipity; or the Literary/Pop Culture Quotes Quiz</b>
</u><br /><br />Late at night, about a week ago, I went through the Top Quotes pages of Goodreads. A hundred pages, each with thirty quotes: that's 3000 quotes. I have problems. I was searching for quotes that included (on the most part, primarily consisted of) a line in the form of 'If A, B' (let's call it an <i>If</i> quote, or IQ) - I'd compiled them because I had a certain idea for a review where I would use them.<br /><br />What's the catch? The idea was for the review of another book - not <i>What If?</i>. But the day after the nighttime scavenging, I decided against using it because my sense of reviewing aesthetics decided to go with another idea. The result was that I had all these IQ's sitting in a file on my desktop, but they were left completely untouched because I didn't plan to use them any more, while regretting how I should have thought about it more before compiling them in the first place.<br /><br />Fast forward a week, and today I finished Munroe's book. I hadn't really thought about reviewing it (immediately, anyway), but tonight I glanced at the book the review of which the IQ's were meant for on my desk, and it hit me.<br /><br /><i>What If?</i> questions. <i>If...</i> quotes. So I'm able to use my dear IQ's for a review after all. Ladies and gentlemen, that's serendipity for you.<br /><br />From the 3000 quotes, there were a surprising amount of IQ's. I'd estimate there were 60 or so quotes including an <i>If...</i> line (citation needed), of which I had copied 45 of into my file for their quality and validity of use in my review for that other book. I'd like to make it 50 for a nicer number but no artificial perfectionism here - science needs integrity.<br /><br />Here's how the IQ's fit into this review:<br />1. I give you the first half of each IQ (the <i>If A</i>).<br />2. You'll also be given the author of the quote.<br />3. Your task is to see how many of the <i>B</i>'s you can fill out. The answers are hidden under the spoiler tags. Better leave peeking at the answer for after you've answered a series by the same author since they might be a giveaway.<br />4. Write your test score /45 in the comments section (give me some love, joy and social validation). For the answers, if they are relatively close you can give yourself the mark.<br />5. Cheaters, if found, will be reported to the Internet Police.<br /><br />Got your pen and paper? Your writing time starts...now.<br /><br />1. If you can't handle me at my worst (Marilyn Monroe [not Randall Munroe]) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[then you sure as hell don't deserve me at my best. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />2. If you only read the books that everyone else is reading (Haruki Murakami) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you can only think what everyone else is thinking. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />3. If you remember me (Haruki Murakami) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[then I don’t care if everyone else forgets. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />4. If there’s a book that you want to read, but it hasn’t been written yet (Toni Morrison) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[then you must write it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />5. If you don't have time to read (Stephen King) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you don’t have the time (or the tools) to write. Simple as that. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />6. If we find ourselves with a desire that nothing in this world can satisfy (C. S. Lewis) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the most probable explanation is that we were made for another world. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />7. If the world were merely seductive (E. B. White) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[that would be easy. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />8. If it were merely challenging (E. B. White) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[that would be no problem. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />9. If more of us valued food and cheer and song above hoarded gold (J. R. R. Tolkien) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[it would be a merrier world. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />10. If a cluttered desk is a sign of a cluttered mind (Albert Einstein) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[of what, then, is an empty desk a sign? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />11. If you have a garden and a library (Marcus Tullius Cicero) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you have everything you need. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />12. If you love somebody (Kahlil Gibran) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[let them go, for if they return, they were always yours. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />13. If you are allergic to a thing (Lemony Snicket) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[it is best not to put that thing in your mouth, particularly if the thing is cats. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />14. If I can stop one heart from breaking (Emily Dickinson) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I shall not live in vain. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />15. If you go home with somebody, and they don’t have books (John Waters) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[don't fuck 'em. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />16. If we wait for the moment when everything, absolutely everything is ready (Ivan Turgenev) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[we shall never begin. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />17. If you don’t like someone’s story (Chinua Achebe) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[write your own. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />18. If you have two friends in your life (S. E. Hinton) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you're lucky. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />19. If you have one good friend (S. E. Hinton) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you're more than lucky. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />20. If I got rid of my demons (Tennessee Williams) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I'd lose my angels. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />21. If I should ever die, God forbid, let this be my epitaph: (Kurt Vonnegut) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[THE ONLY PROOF HE NEEDED <br />FOR THE EXISTENCE OF GOD<br />WAS MUSIC <br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />22. If she's amazing (Bob Marley) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[she won't be easy. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />23. If she's easy (Bob Marley) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[she won't be amazing. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />24. If she' s worth it (Bob Marley) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you won't give up. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />25. If you give up (Bob Marley) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you're not worthy. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />26. If you're losing your soul and you know it (Charles Bukowski) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[then you’ve still got a soul left to lose.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />27. If you are irritated by every rub (Jalāl ad-Dīn Muhammad Rūmī) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[how will your mirror be polished? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />28. If you ask me what I came to do in this world (Émile Zola) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I, an artist, will answer you: I am here to live out loud.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />29. If you pick up a starving dog and make him prosperous (Mark Twain) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he will not bite you. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />30. If you do not tell the truth about yourself (Virginia Woolf) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you cannot tell it about other people. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />31. If you treat an individual as he is (Johann Wolfgang von Goethe) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he will remain how he is. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />32. If you treat him as if he were what he ought to be and could be (Johann Wolfgang von Goethe) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he will become what he ought to be and could be.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />33. If you don't read the newspaper (Mark Twain) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you're uninformed. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />34. If you read the newspaper (Mark Twain) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you're misinformed. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />35. If the whole universe has no meaning (C. S. Lewis) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[we should never have found out that it has no meaning. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />36. If I had a world of my own (Lewis Carroll) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[everything would be nonsense. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />37. If we couldn't laugh (Robert Frost) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[we would all go insane. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />38. If you think you are too small to make a difference (Dalai Lama XIV) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[try sleeping with a mosquito. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />39. If you want to know what God thinks of money (Dorothy Parker) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[just look at the people he gave it to. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />40. If you liked being a teenager (Stephen King) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[there's something really wrong with you. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />41. If you are lonely when you’re alone (Jean-Paul Sartre) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[you're in bad company. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />42. If, then, I were asked for the most important advice I could give, that which I considered to be the most useful to the men of our country, I should simply say: (Leo Tolstoy) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[in the name of God, stop a moment, cease your work, look around you. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />43. If I am not good to myself (Maya Angelou) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[how can I expect anyone else to be good to me?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />44. If a man will begin with certainties (Francis Bacon) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he shall end in doubts. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />45. If he will be content to begin with doubts (Francis Bacon) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he shall end in certainties. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Well, how did you go? Even if you're too shy or lazy to post your score, at least you will have learnt some new quotes.<br /><br />Isn't one of the questions that leads to progress in science, <i>What If...</i>? Munroe's book is filled with dozens and dozens of curious questions, and it's highly recommended for<br />a) <a href="http://www.xkcd.com" rel="nofollow noopener">xkcd</a> fans<br />b) those with an interest in science<br />c) geeks<br />d) anyone curious about the universe<br /><br />And I'm pretty sure most of you would fit into one of these categories.<br /><br /><br />November 16, 2015<br /><br />P.S. My own score for the quiz would have been 19.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TLT5QpqEsmg4ItvJCwQ+eTDFUF+81K8RP3v5xeR3F20Saaho6hbVuRIIUYwTMO9LEvdQOPNpsWL3WM8UocBQOQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('K+FJZFYGtOvIqBiMDqo9kzPmgrRdSbn2kPtCShZCwJh1PBhOJpTTOuKCkskWnuyhEdSC0xL0p4VY2HSbU/WHzA==')})
  }

Suppose, instead of thinking about organisms using genes to reproduce themselves, as we had since Mendel\'s work was rediscovered, we turn it around and imagine that &quot;our&quot; genes build and maintain us in order to make more genes. Drawing fascinating examples from every field of biology, he paved the way for a serious re-evaluation of evolution. To that end, he has attached himself to a host of the world’s most advanced (and often obsessed) archaeologists, anthropologists, and mathematicians, travelling to their offices, laboratories, and field camps. He has read (or tried to read) their books, pestered them with questions, apprenticed himself to their powerful minds. A Short History of Nearly Everything is the record of this quest, and it is a sometimes profound, sometimes funny, and always supremely clear and entertaining adventure in the realms of human knowledge, as only Bill Bryson can render it.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_8520362'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8520362-the-grand-design?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Grand Design<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/1401.Stephen_Hawking?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Stephen Hawking<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.04 avg rating &mdash; 64,059 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2010\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer12884739791706487850\">THE FIRST MAJOR WORK IN NEARLY A DECADE BY ONE OF THE WORLD’S GREAT THINKERS—A MARVELOUSLY CONCISE BOOK WITH NEW ANSWERS TO THE ULTIMATE QUESTIONS OF LIFE When and how did the universe begin? Why are we here? Why is there something rather than nothin<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12884739791706487850\" style=\"display:none\">THE FIRST MAJOR WORK IN NEARLY A DECADE BY ONE OF THE WORLD’S GREAT THINKERS—A MARVELOUSLY CONCISE BOOK WITH NEW ANSWERS TO THE ULTIMATE QUESTIONS OF LIFE When and how did the universe begin? Why are we here? Why is there something rather than nothing? What is the nature of reality? Why are the laws of nature so finely tuned as to allow for the existence of beings like ourselves? And, finally, is the apparent “grand design” of our universe evidence of a benevolent creator who set things in motion—or does science offer another explanation? The most fundamental questions about the origins of the universe and of life itself, once the province of philosophy, now occupy the territory where scientists, philosophers, and theologians meet—if only to disagree. In their new book, Stephen Hawking and Leonard Mlodinow present the most recent scientific thinking about the mysteries of the universe, in nontechnical language marked by both brilliance and simplicity. In The Grand Design they explain that according to quantum theory, the cosmos does not have just a single existence or history, but rather that every possible history of the universe exists simultaneously. When applied to the universe as a whole, this idea calls into question the very notion of cause and effect. But the “top-down” approach to cosmology that Hawking and Mlodinow describe would say that the fact that the past takes no definite form means that we create history by observing it, rather than that history creates us. The authors further explain that we ourselves are the product of quantum fluctuations in the very early universe, and show how quantum theory predicts the “multiverse”—the idea that ours is just one of many universes that appeared spontaneously out of nothing, each with different laws of nature.Along the way Hawking and Mlodinow question the conventional concept of reality, posing a “model-dependent” theory of reality as the best we can hope to find. And they conclude with a riveting assessment of M-theory, an explanation of the laws governing us and our universe that is currently the only viable candidate for a complete “theory of everything.” If confirmed, they write, it will be the unified theory that Einstein was looking for, and the ultimate triumph of human reason.A succinct, startling, and lavishly illustrated guide to discoveries that are altering our understanding and threatening some of our most cherished belief systems, The Grand Design is a book that will inform—and provoke—like no other.\'<\/span>\n  <a Until very recently, science had no answer to the question of why we sleep, or what good it served, or why we suffer such devastating health consequences when we don\'t sleep. Compared to the other basic drives in life—eating, drinking, and reproducing—the purpose of sleep remained elusive.  An explosion of scientific discoveries in the last twenty years has shed new light on this fundamental aspect of our lives. Now, preeminent neuroscientist and sleep expert Matthew Walker gives us a new understanding of the vital importance of sleep and dreaming. Within the brain, sleep enriches our ability to learn, memorize, and make logical decisions. It recalibrates our emotions, restocks our immune system, fine-tunes our metabolism, and regulates our appetite. Dreaming mollifies painful memories and creates a virtual reality space in which the brain melds past and present knowledge to inspire creativity.  Walker answers important questions about sleep: how do caffeine and alcohol affect sleep? What really happens during REM sleep? Why do our sleep patterns change across a lifetime? How do common sleep aids affect us and can they do long-term damage? Charting cutting-edge scientific breakthroughs, and synthesizing decades of research and clinical practice, Walker explains how we can harness sleep to improve learning, mood, and energy levels; regulate hormones; prevent cancer, Alzheimer’s, and diabetes; slow the effects of aging; increase longevity; enhance the education and lifespan of our children, and boost the efficiency, success, and productivity of our businesses. System 1 is fast, intuitive, and emotional; System 2 is slower, more deliberative, and more logical. Kahneman exposes the extraordinary capabilities—and also the faults and biases—of fast thinking, and reveals the pervasive influence of intuitive impressions on our thoughts and behavior. The impact of loss aversion and overconfidence on corporate strategies, the difficulties of predicting what will make us happy in the future, the challenges of properly framing risks at work and at home, the profound effect of cognitive biases on everything from playing the stock market to planning the next vacation—each of these can be understood only by knowing how the two systems work together to shape our judgments and decisions.\n\nEngaging the reader in a lively conversation about how we think, Kahneman reveals where we can and cannot trust our intuitions and how we can tap into the benefits of slow thinking. Darwin was only too aware of the storm his theory of evolution would provoke but he would surely have raised an incredulous eyebrow at the controversy still raging a century and a half later. Evolution is accepted as scientific fact by all reputable scientists and indeed theologians, yet millions of people continue to question its veracity.\n\nIn The Greatest Show on Earth Richard Dawkins takes on creationists, including followers of ‘Intelligent Design’ and all those who question the fact of evolution through natural selection. Like a detective arriving on the scene of a crime, he sifts through fascinating layers of scientific facts and disciplines to build a cast-iron case: from the living examples of natural selection in birds and insects; the ‘time clocks’ of trees and radioactive dating that calibrate a timescale for evolution; the fossil record and the traces of our earliest ancestors; to confirmation from molecular biology and genetics. All of this, and much more, bears witness to the truth of evolution.\n\nThe Greatest Show on Earth comes at a critical time: systematic opposition to the fact of evolution is now flourishing as never before, especially in America. In Britain and elsewhere in the world, teachers witness insidious attempts to undermine the status of science in their classrooms. Richard Dawkins provides unequivocal evidence that boldly and comprehensively rebuts such nonsense. At the same time he shares with us his palpable love of the natural world and the essential role that science plays in its interpretation. Written with elegance, wit and passion, it is hard-hitting, absorbing and totally convincing.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13762770897775399963\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return He eviscerates the major arguments for religion, and demonstrates the supreme improbability of a supreme being. He shows how religion fuels war, foments bigotry, and abuses children, buttressing his points with historical and contemporary evidence.\n\nThe God Delusion makes a compelling case that belief in God is not just wrong, but potentially deadly. It also offers exhilarating insight into the advantages of atheism to the individual and society, not the least of which is a clearer, truer appreciation of the universe\'s wonders than any faith       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3089156.Randall_Munroe">About Randall Munroe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3089156.Randall_Munroe"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1406058780p3/3089156.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3089156.Randall_Munroe">Randall Munroe
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer888473887626258200">Randall Munroe, a former NASA roboticist, is the creator of the webcomic xkcd and the author of xkcd: volume 0. The International Astronomical Union recently named an asteroid after him; asteroid 4942 Munroe is big enough to cause a mass extinction if it ever hits a planet like Earth. He lives in Cambridge, Massachusetts. </span>

</div>
